Tenth Proof: Refuting Spatial Relations with the Throne
A proof refuting three possibilities: contact, finite separation, or infinite separation from the Throne.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how classical kalam refutes three possible spatial relations between God and the Throne, establishing divine transcendence beyond physical categories.

Core Idea
God's relation to the Throne is neither contact, finite separation, nor infinite separation.

This passage presents a proof that all logical possibilities for a spatial relationship between God and the Throne are impossible. By systematically eliminating contact, finite distance, and infinite distance, the author demonstrates that God cannot be 'above' the Throne in any physical sense. This reinforces the doctrine of tanzih (divine transcendence).

Guided Path
1
Learn the three divisions
The author begins by stating three exhaustive possibilities: God above the Throne (in contact), separated by finite distance, or separated by infinite distance. Recognize that these are considered mutually exclusive and collectively exhaustive.
The Arabic uses 'فوق العرش' and 'مباينة' with different forms.
2
Analyze the refutation of contact
Read the first refutation carefully. It argues that contact would require God to have parts like the Throne, and that this would allow the Throne itself to be considered a god. Understand the reductio ad absurdum.
The argument uses analogy and the principle that an attribute leading to negation of the described is false.
3
Analyze the refutation of finite separation
The second refutation argues that finite separation implies alignment and the possibility of eventual contact if the world moves upward. This also leads to absurdity.
Note the terms 'محاذاة', 'موازاة', 'مقابلة'.
4
Consider the incomplete third refutation
The third division (infinite separation) is only partially refuted. The text begins to argue that the distance would be bounded, but cuts off. Reflect on why the author considers this impossible as well.
The argument likely continues that infinite separation would require an infinite distance between two finite entities, which is contradictory.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة العاشرة : لو كان تعالى فوق العرش ، أو مباينا للعرش ببينونة متناهية ، أو مباينا له ببينونة غير متناهية. والأقسام الثلاثة باطلة فالقول بكونه فوق العرش قول باطل . أما الحصر فظاهر . وأما بيان أنه يمتنع أن يكون مماسا للعرش : فالدليل عليه : أن العرش مؤلف من الأجزاء والأبعاض ، فالذي يماسه هذا الجزء من العرش ، غير الذي يماسه الجزء الثاني من العرش ، وكما أن العرش مؤلف من الأجزاء والأبعاض ، وجب أن يكون الله تعالى مؤلفا من الأجزاء والأبعاض . وأيضاً : فلو عقل بأن يكون المؤلف من الأجزاء والأبعاض إلهاً ، فلم لا يجوز أن يقال : إن إله العالم هو العرش نفسه ؟ لأنه لو جاز أن يقال : إن الإله مؤلف من الأجزاء والأبعاض ، مع أنه لا يقبل التفرق والتمزق ، فلم لا يجوز أن يقال : العرش وإن كان مؤلفا من الأجزاء والأبعاض إلا أنه لا يقبل التفرق والتمزق ومعلوم أن فتح هذا الباب يفضي إلى نفي ، وكل صفة يفضي إثباتها إلى نفي الموصوف فهو باطل فيثبت : أن القول بكون الإله مماسا للعرش قول باطل . الصانع . وأما القسم الثاني وهو أنه تعالى مباين للعرش ببينونة متناهية : فهذا أيضا باطل ، لأن على هذا التقدير تكون ذات الله تعالى محاذية لذات العرش ، وموازية لها ، وواقعة في مقابلتها ، وكل ما ذكرناه في المماسة فهو بعينه قائم في هذه المحاذات . وأيضا : فعلى هذا التقدير لا يمتنع أن يرتفع العالم من حيزه إلى الجانب الأعلى ارتفاعا متناهيا وحينئذ يلزم صحة أن تصير ذات الله مماسا للعرش . وأما القسم الثالث وهو أن يكون مباينا للعرش ببينونة غير متناهية : فهذا أيضا باطل ، لأنه إذا كان العالم من أحد الجانبين وكانت ذات الله من الجانب الآخر ، فحينئذ يكون البعد بينهما محدودا بهذين الطرفين ، فلو قلنا : إن
The tenth proof: If the Exalted were above the Throne, or separated from the Throne by a finite separation, or separated from it by an infinite separation. And the three divisions are false, so the statement that He is above the Throne is false. As for the enumeration, it is apparent. As for the demonstration that it is impossible for Him to be in contact with the Throne, the proof for it is: that the Throne is composed of parts and limbs; that which touches it from this part of the Throne is other than that which touches it from the second part of the Throne. And just as the Throne is composed of parts and limbs, it is necessary that God the Exalted be composed of parts and limbs. Also: if it could be understood that the one composed of parts and limbs is a god, then why is it not permissible to say that the God of the world is the Throne itself? Because if it is permissible to say that the god is composed of parts and limbs despite not accepting separation and disintegration, why is it not permissible to say that the Throne, even though composed of parts and limbs, does not accept separation and disintegration? It is known that opening this door leads to negation, and every attribute whose affirmation leads to the negation of the described is false. Thus it is established that the statement that the god is in contact with the Throne is false. (The word 'الصانع' appears here.) As for the second division, that He is separated from the Throne by a finite separation, this is also false, because on this supposition the essence of God would be aligned with the essence of the Throne, parallel to it, and opposite to it. And everything we mentioned regarding contact applies exactly to this alignment. Also: on this supposition, it is not impossible that the world rises from its location to the higher side a finite rise, and then it would follow the validity of the essence of God becoming in contact with the Throne. As for the third division, that He is separated from the Throne by an infinite separation, this is also false, because if the world is on one side and the essence of God is on the other side, then the distance between them would be bounded by these two ends. So if we were to say that...
Source pages p. 51

Commentary
This passage is the tenth proof, which enumerates and refutes three possible ways God could be related to the Throne (العرش). The author begins by stating that only three possibilities exist: (1) God is above and in contact with the Throne, (2) He is separated by a finite distance, or (3) He is separated by an infinite distance. The proof then goes on to refute each. The first division (contact) is refuted by two arguments: first, if God were in contact, He would have to have parts like the Throne; second, this would imply that the Throne itself could be a god, which is absurd. The second division (finite separation) is refuted because it implies alignment and the possibility of eventual contact if the world moves upward. The third division (infinite separation) is only partially refuted in the existing text; the argument begins by stating that the distance would be bounded by the two ends, implying a contradiction, but the text cuts off before completing the refutation. Nonetheless, the author's conclusion is that all three are false, so God cannot be above or separated from the Throne in any spatial sense.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that God being 'above the Throne' is a literal spatial position that can be understood through contact or distance.
Correction: The proof shows that all spatial possibilities lead to absurdities, so 'above' must be understood metaphorically, not physically.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The only consistent position is that God transcends spatial dimensions; any physical relation to the Throne is impossible.

The Tenth Argument: Refuting Spatial Relation to the Throne
Al-Razi presents the tenth argument with three divisions to prove God's transcendence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Fakhr al-Din al-Razi uses an exhaustive division of possibilities to prove that Allah is not spatially related to the throne.

Core Idea
Allah is beyond space and direction; any spatial link to creation is impossible.

Al-Razi's tenth argument is a masterful use of rational proof to establish divine transcendence. He begins by enumerating every conceivable spatial relation between Allah and the throne, then systematically shows each to be impossible. This lesson guides you through his reasoning so you can see why the only consistent position is that Allah is free from place and direction.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the entire argument once to get the flow. Notice the three divisions and the conclusion.
The passage is a single continuous argument.
2
Identify the three divisions
Al-Razi presents three possible spatial relations: touching, finite separation, infinite separation. Find where he states these.
These are the three cases he refutes.
3
Analyze the refutation of touching
Examine the two reasons he gives against the first division: (a) composition of Allah would follow, and (b) it would allow the throne to be god.
Think about why composition is impossible for God.
4
Analyze the refutation of finite separation
Al-Razi argues that finite separation leads to adjacency and parallelism, inviting the same composition problem, and also allows the world to potentially reach God.
This shows the absurdity of a finite gap.
5
Analyze the refutation of infinite separation
He argues that an infinite distance bound by two endpoints is impossible because infinite cannot be enclosed.
This final refutation completes the argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة العاشرة : لو كان تعالى فوق العرش ، أو مباينا للعرش ببينونة متناهية ، أو مباينا له ببينونة غير متناهية. والأقسام الثلاثة باطلة فالقول بكونه فوق العرش قول باطل . أما الحصر فظاهر . وأما بيان أنه يمتنع أن يكون مماسا للعرش : فالدليل عليه : أن العرش مؤلف من الأجزاء والأبعاض ، فالذي يماسه هذا الجزء من العرش ، غير الذي يماسه الجزء الثاني من العرش ، وكما أن العرش مؤلف من الأجزاء والأبعاض ، وجب أن يكون الله تعالى مؤلفا من الأجزاء والأبعاض . وأيضاً : فلو عقل بأن يكون المؤلف من الأجزاء والأبعاض إلهاً ، فلم لا يجوز أن يقال : إن إله العالم هو العرش نفسه ؟ لأنه لو جاز أن يقال : إن الإله مؤلف من الأجزاء والأبعاض ، مع أنه لا يقبل التفرق والتمزق ، فلم لا يجوز أن يقال : العرش وإن كان مؤلفا من الأجزاء والأبعاض إلا أنه لا يقبل التفرق والتمزق ومعلوم أن فتح هذا الباب يفضي إلى نفي ، وكل صفة يفضي إثباتها إلى نفي الموصوف فهو باطل فيثبت : أن القول بكون الإله مماسا للعرش قول باطل . وأما القسم الثاني وهو أنه تعالى مباين للعرش ببينونة متناهية : فهذا أيضا باطل ، لأن على هذا التقدير تكون ذات الله تعالى محاذية لذات العرش ، وموازية لها ، وواقعة في مقابلتها ، وكل ما ذكرناه في المماسة فهو بعينه قائم في هذه المحاذات وأيضا : فعلى هذا التقدير لا يمتنع أن يرتفع العالم من حيزه إلى الجانب الأعلى ارتفاعا متناهيا وحينئذ يلزم صحة أن تصير ذات الله مماسا للعرش . وأما القسم الثالث وهو أن يكون مباينا للعرش ببينونة غير متناهية : فهذا أيضا باطل ، لأنه إذا كان العالم من أحد الجانبين وكانت ذات الله من الجانب الآخر ، فحينئذ يكون البعد بينهما محدودا بهذين الطرفين ، فلو قلنا : إن هذا البعد غير متناه ، لزم أن يقال : إن مالا نهاية له محصور بين حاصرتين وذلك محال . فثبت فساد هذه الأقسام الثلاثة ، ويلزم من فسادها القول بتنزيه ذات الله تعالى عن المكان والحيز.
The tenth argument: If the Exalted were above the throne, or separate from the throne with a finite separation, or separate from it with an infinite separation. The three divisions are false, so the statement that He is above the throne is a false statement. As for the enumeration, it is apparent. As for the demonstration that it is impossible for Him to be touching the throne: the proof for it is that the throne is composed of parts and members; that which touches it from this part of the throne is other than that which touches it from the second part of the throne; and just as the throne is composed of parts and members, it would be necessary that Allah the Exalted be composed of parts and members. Also: If it could be rationally conceived that one composed of parts and members be a god, then why is it not permissible to say that the god of the world is the throne itself? Because if it were permissible to say that the god is composed of parts and members, while He does not accept division and fragmentation, then why is it not permissible to say that the throne, even though composed of parts and members, does not accept division and fragmentation? And it is known that opening this door leads to negation, and every attribute whose affirmation leads to the negation of the described is false; thus it is established that the statement that the god is touching the throne is a false statement. As for the second division, namely that He the Exalted is separate from the throne with a finite separation: this too is false, because on this supposition the essence of Allah the Exalted would be adjacent to the essence of the throne, parallel to it, and situated opposite it; and everything we mentioned concerning touching applies exactly to this adjacency. Also: on this supposition it is not impossible for the world to rise from its place to the higher side a finite ascent, and then it would necessitate the correctness that the essence of Allah become touching the throne. As for the third division, namely that He be separate from the throne with an infinite separation: this too is false, because if the world is on one side and the essence of Allah on the other side, then the distance between them would be limited by these two boundaries; if we say that this distance is infinite, it would require that that which has no end be confined between two confiners, and that is impossible. Thus the falsity of these three divisions is established, and from their falsity follows the statement of transcending the essence of Allah the Exalted from place and direction.
Source pages p. 51

Commentary
This passage presents the tenth argument (al-ḥujja al-ʿāshira) from Fakhr al-Din al-Razi's work, refuting the claim that Allah is above the throne or in any spatial relation to it. The argument begins by stating three exhaustive possibilities: (1) Allah is touching the throne, (2) Allah is separate from the throne with a finite distance, or (3) Allah is separate from the throne with an infinite distance. The author then demonstrates the impossibility of each case. For the first case, he argues that if Allah touches the throne, then since the throne is composed of parts, that which touches one part is not what touches another part, implying composition in Allah—which is impossible. Further, if composition were allowable for a deity, the throne itself could be considered a god, leading to absurd consequences. For the second case, finite separation implies adjacency and parallelism, which reintroduces the same composition argument; additionally, it would allow the world to potentially reach Allah by ascending, making Him touchable—contradicting His transcendence. For the third case, infinite separation would entail an infinite distance bounded by two endpoints (the world and Allah), which is logically impossible. Therefore, all three possibilities are false, necessitating the conclusion that Allah is free from place and direction (tanzīh ʿan al-makān wa al-ḥayyiz). The passage is a clear example of rational proof (dalīl ʿaqlī) in kalām, establishing divine transcendence through exhaustive division and refutation of each alternative.

Key Terms
مماس
مباين
بينونة
العرش
الحيز
تنزيه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument denies any form of 'above' for Allah, including the Qur'anic 'istiwa'.
Correction: Al-Razi only refutes spatial location; the divine attribute of istiwa is affirmed in a non-spatial manner.

Checkpoint
What are the three possible relations to the throne that al-Razi refutes in the tenth argument?
A) Touching, finite separation, infinite separation
B) Above, below, beside
C) Essential, accidental, relational
D) Necessary, impossible, possible

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi's tenth argument conclusively shows that Allah cannot be spatially related to the throne; He is free from place and direction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you picture Allah in your mind, do you subconsciously assign a direction or location? How does this argument expose that as a subtle form of limitation? What concrete step will you take today to train your mind to think of Allah as beyond space?
?
If you previously believed that Allah is 'above' the throne in a spatial sense, how does this argument challenge that belief? Are you willing to surrender a comfortable mental image for the sake of tanzih, even if it feels less tangible? What will you do when the urge to imagine a spatial God returns?
?
Think of a time you felt Allah was 'far' from you because of your sins. Did that feeling involve a sense of distance? How does this lesson redefine 'distance' from Allah as a spiritual state, not a spatial one? What will you change about how you seek nearness to Him from now on?

First Objection: The Possibility of Touching the Throne
The objector questions the impossibility of Allah touching the throne, proposing an alternative and accusing the author of illicit analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objector's argument that Allah could be a simple entity present everywhere, and why the objector claims the author's reasoning wrongly analogizes physical atoms to God.

Core Idea
The objector turns the table, accusing the author of illicit analogy from physical atoms to God.

In this passage, an imaginary objector challenges the author's earlier claim that touching the throne is impossible. The objector proposes that Allah, unlike physical atoms, could be a single simple entity present in all places at once, touching all throne parts simultaneously. He then argues that the author's argument rests on an illegitimate analogy between the unseen (God) and the witnessed (physical atoms). This is a classic kalām dialectical move.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and response
Read the Arabic passage and translation, noting the structure of question and answer.
The objector's speech is marked by "فإن قيل" and continues until the end of the passage.
2
Identify the objector's main claim
Notice the objector's suggestion that Allah could be one thing present in all places, touching all parts of the throne simultaneously.
This is a counter-proposal to the author's composition argument.
3
Notice the admission about atoms
The objector concedes that this kind of omnipresence is impossible for a physical atom (jawhar fard), but argues that this does not apply to Allah.
The objector is distinguishing between physical and divine natures.
4
Understand the qiyās critique
The objector accuses the author of using an invalid analogy from the physical to the divine, which the author's own school rejects.
This methodological critique is central to kalām debates on divine attributes.
5
Reflect on the dialectical context
Recognize that this is a hypothetical objection, not the author's own view. The author's response would follow in subsequent lines.
In kalām, objections (shubuhāt) are presented and then refuted.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : لم لا يجوز أن يقال : إنه مماس للعرش ، أو مباين له بينونة متناهية ؟ قوله : « إنه لو كان كذلك ، لكان الذي يماس منه أحد أجزاء العرش ، مغاير للذي يماس منه الجزء الثاني ، قلنا : لا نسلم . ولم لا يجوز أن يقال : إنه شيء واحد ، وهو بعينه حاصلا في جميع الأحياز ، وهو بعينه يماس جميع أجزاء العرش ، أو يحاذيها ؟ وأقصى ما في الباب أن هذا في الجوهر الفرد محال ، فإن الجوهر الواحد لا يحصل في الأحياز الكثيرة دفعة واحدة . فلم قلتم : إنه لما كان الأمر كذلك في الجوهر ، وجب أن يكون الأمر كذلك في حق الباري تعالى ؟ فإن قياس الغائب على الشاهد مطلقاً باطل ، لا سيما على مذهبكم فإن الأصل المعتبر في إثبات التنزيه أن قياس الغائب على الشاهد لا يجوز .
If it is said: 'Why is it not permissible to say that He is touching the throne, or separate from it with a finite separation?' His saying: 'If it were so, then that which touches it from one part of the throne would be different from that which touches it from the second part.' We say: 'We do not accept that. And why is it not permissible to say that He is one thing, and it is exactly the same thing that is present in all places, and it is the very same that touches all parts of the throne or is adjacent to them? The utmost in this matter is that this is impossible for the indivisible atom (jawhar fard), for a single atom cannot be present in many places at once. But why do you say that because it is the case with the atom, it must be the case with the Exalted Creator? For analogizing the absent (al-ghā'ib) to the present (al-shāhid) is absolutely invalid, especially according to your school, for the established principle in affirming transcendence is that analogizing the absent to the present is not permissible.'
Source pages p. 52

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer unit. The objector questions why Allah cannot be touching or finitely separate from the throne. The objector denies the premise that if Allah touches one part, He must be different when touching another part. Instead, the objector suggests that Allah could be a single, simple entity that is simultaneously present in all places and touches all parts of the throne at once. The objector acknowledges that this is impossible for a physical atom (jawhar fard)—a single atom cannot be in multiple places simultaneously—but argues that this does not apply to Allah because Allah is not a physical atom. The objector accuses the author of illegitimately analogizing the unseen (al-ghā'ib) to the witnessed (al-shāhid), which the author's own school rejects when it leads to anthropomorphism. The objector's point is that the impossibility derived from physical atoms does not necessarily apply to God, so the argument against touching the throne is not valid. Note that this is the objector's statement; the author's response would follow, but the lesson only includes the objection and the beginning of the response (the author says 'قلنا' but then the objector's continuation? Actually the text after 'قلنا' is the response, but the objector presents a counterargument. The author's full response likely continues, but the lesson as extracted ends with the objector's point about qiyās al-ghā'ib 'alā al-shāhid. This is a common kalām dialectical format.

Infinite Separation: Defending the Possibility
Objector defends infinite distance by analogy to God's temporal infinity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the objector uses an analogy from God's eternal existence to argue that infinite spatial separation between God and the throne is not inherently impossible.

Core Idea
Temporal infinity without absurdity suggests spatial infinity may also be possible.

The debate over God's location continues with a clever objection. The opponent concedes that finite separation leads to absurdity, but he does not concede defeat. Instead, he shifts the ground: what if the separation is infinite? He then presents an analogical argument from God's relationship with time. By examining this objection, we refine our understanding of what kalam considers rationally necessary or impossible.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Pay attention to the concession phrase 'sallamnā' and the shift to the new argument.
2
Identify the two moves
The objector first concedes that finite separation leads to impossibility, then argues that infinite separation does not. Then he introduces an analogy from God's eternity.
3
Analyze the analogy
The objector compares God's separation from the throne to God's separation from temporal events in pre-eternity. Understand the parallel: infinite separation in time (azal) does not create absurdity, so why not in space?
4
Reflect on the limitation
Notice that the objector's concession strengthens the overall argument for divine transcendence (tanzih). The commentary warns against misinterpreting the objection as asserting spatial infinite distance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
سلمنا أن ذلك باطل إلا أن هذا إنما يلزم ، إذا قلنا : إنه تعالى محاذ للعرش ببعد متناه أما إذا قلنا : إنه مباين للعرش ببعد غير متناه فهذا غير لازم . أما قوله ( لو كان كذلك ، لزم أن يكون ما لا نهاية له محصورابين حاصرين ، وذلك محال ، قلنا : أليس أن الله تعالى قديم أزلي ؟ فعلى هذا أنه لا أول لوجوده في جانب الأزل ، أما وجوده من الجانب الذي يلينا فإنه مقارن لوجود كل واحد من هذه الحوادث ، فإذا جاز في المدة والزمان أن تكون ذات الله مباينة لهذه الحوادث، من جانب الأزل بينونة غير متناهية، مع أنه يكون مقارناً للعالم في جانب الأبد . فلم لا يجوز أيضا أن يقال : إن ذاته مباينة للعرش ببعد غير متناه ؟
We concede that that is false, but this only follows if we say that He the Exalted is adjacent to the throne with a finite distance; but if we say that He is separate from the throne with an infinite distance, this does not follow. As for His saying: 'If it were so, then that which has no end would be confined between two confiners, and that is impossible.' We say: 'Is not Allah the Exalted eternal (qadīm azalī)? Thus, there is no beginning to His existence on the side of pre-eternity (al-azal). As for His existence on the side that is toward us, it is concomitant with the existence of each one of these accidents. So if it is permissible in duration and time that the essence of Allah be separate from these accidents from the side of pre-eternity with an infinite separation, while He is concomitant with the world on the side of eternity (al-abad), then why is it not also permissible to say that His essence is separate from the throne with an infinite distance?'
Source pages p. 52

Commentary
This passage contains a double objection. First, the objector concedes (sallamnā) that the third division (infinite separation) is false but argues that this falsity only follows if we assume finite separation; the earlier refutation of finite separation used a different reasoning (leading to touching), but the objector seems to differentiate. Then the objector presents a new argument: the author's claim that an infinite distance bounded by two ends is impossible is challenged by drawing an analogy to God's relationship with time. The objector says: God is eternal (qadīm azalī) — He has no beginning on the side of pre-eternity (al-azal), yet His existence is simultaneous with the temporal events (ḥawādith) on the side that faces us (i.e., from the creation onward). So His existence is separate (mubāyina) from these events on the azal side with an infinite separation, while He is concurrent with them on the abad side (eternal future). This is an infinite separation bounded by? Actually, the objector argues that this is an infinite separation (in time) that does not involve absurdity, so why cannot the spatial infinite separation also be acceptable? This is a clever analogy trying to show that infinite separation between two endpoints (God and the world) is not inherently impossible, as it is analogous to God's temporal relation to events.

Correction: The objector is not asserting that; he is using the possibility of infinite separation in time to argue that spatial infinite separation is not inherently impossible, thereby defending his position.

Key Takeaway

God's Transcendence: Answering the Objection of Contact
Concludes God is beyond place then answers the objection about contact or finite separation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the author rejects the objection that God could be in contact with the Throne or separate by a finite distance, and the principle that analogical reasoning from the created to the Creator is invalid.

Core Idea
God cannot be compared to created objects; analogy from the created is invalid for the Creator.

Having established that God is beyond place and space, an objector raises a hypothetical: could God be touching the Throne or separated by a finite distance? The author counters by showing the flawed analogy behind this question. This passage introduces a key principle in Islamic theology: analogical reasoning from the created to the Creator is absolutely invalid.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Read the objection: why might someone think God could be touching or finitely separate? The objector uses an analogy from the indivisible atom.
The objector argues that if God can be present in all places, He could touch the Throne entirely.
2
Analyze the author's response
The author first denies the premise (لا نسلم) and then shows the absurdity of applying atomic properties to God.
Notice the author uses reductio: if God touched parts, He would have parts.
3
Learn the key principle
The author concludes that analogical reasoning from the absent (غائب) to the present (شاهد) is invalid, especially in divine matters.
This principle is foundational for divine transcendence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فثبت فساد هذه الأقسام الثلاثة ، ويلزم من فسادها القول بتنزيه ذات الله تعالى عن المكان والحيز . فإن قيل : لم لا يجوز أن يقال : إنه مماس للعرش ، أو مباين له بينونة متناهية ؟ قوله : « إنه لو كان كذلك ، لكان الذي يماس منه أحد أجزاء العرش ، مغاير للذي يماس منه الجزء الثاني ، قلنا : لا نسلم . ولم لا يجوز أن يقال : إنه شيء واحد ، وهو بعينه حاصلا في جميع الأحياز ، وهو بعينه يماس جميع أجزاء العرش ، أو يحاذيها ؟ وأقصى ما في الباب أن هذا في الجوهر الفرد محال ، فإن الجوهر الواحد لا يحصل في الأحياز الكثيرة دفعة واحدة . فلم قلتم : إنه لما كان الأمر كذلك في الجوهر ، وجب أن يكون الأمر كذلك في حق الباري تعالى ؟ فإن قياس الغائب على الشاهد مطلقاً باطل ، لا سيما على مذهبكم فإن الأصل المعتبر في إثبات التنزيه أن قياس الغائب على الشاهد لا يجوز .
Thus the corruption of these three divisions is established, and from their corruption follows the statement of exalting the essence of God Almighty above place and space. If it is said: 'Why is it not permissible to say that He is in contact with the Throne, or separate from it by a finite separation?' His saying: 'If it were so, then that which touches one part of the Throne would be different from that which touches the second part.' We say: 'We do not accept. And why is it not permissible to say that He is one thing, and He Himself is present in all spaces, and He Himself touches all parts of the Throne, or is opposite to them? The extent of the matter is that this is impossible in the indivisible atom (al-jawhar al-fard), for one indivisible atom does not occur in many spaces at once. So why do you say that because the matter is so in the atom, it must be so in respect of the Exalted Creator? For indeed, analogical reasoning from the absent to the present (qiyās al-ghāʾib ʿalā al-shāhid) is absolutely invalid, especially according to your doctrine, for the established principle in proving transcendence is that analogical reasoning from the absent to the present is not permissible.'
Source pages p. 52

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer unit. The author first states that the falsity of three previously discussed divisions entails affirming God's transcendence above place and direction. Then an objector asks: why can't God be touching the Throne or separate by a finite distance? The author quotes the objector's argument: if God touches different parts of the Throne, He would have to have parts. Then the author responds by rejecting that premise: 'We do not accept.' The objector further suggests that God could be a single entity present in all places simultaneously. The author counters that this is impossible for an indivisible atom (jawhar fard) – a single atom cannot be in multiple places at once. But the objector might analogize from the atom to God. The author refutes this analogy, insisting that analogical reasoning from the created (shāhid) to the Creator (ghāʾib) is absolutely invalid, especially on the objector's own principles of transcendence.

مماسة
مباينة
جوهر فرد
قياس الغائب على الشاهد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that denying contact with the Throne implies God is not present anywhere.
Correction: The passage clarifies that the issue is about specific spatial contact, not absolute presence; God is present without being limited to a place.

Checkpoint

A) The atom is indivisible but God is not.
B) Analogical reasoning from the absent to the present is absolutely invalid.
C) The atom can be in multiple places but God cannot.
D) The objector has not proven that God is one indivisible atom.

Key Takeaway
Analogical reasoning from created objects to God is invalid; God's transcendence means He cannot be compared to any created thing.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think about a time you assumed God must act or feel like a human because that's all you know. How has that assumption quietly shaped your expectations of Him? Are you willing to let go of that comfort and trust His transcendence, even if it feels abstract?
?
The passage says analogical reasoning from the created to the Creator is invalid. In your daily life, when do you find yourself unconsciously comparing God to something or someone else? What would it cost you to stop that comparison and simply submit to His unique nature?
?
If God cannot be contained by space, yet He is closer to you than your own vein (Quran 50:16), how should that change the way you seek Him? Are you ready to abandon the need to 'place' Him and instead pursue a relationship based on His revealed attributes alone?

The Objection of Inseparable Parts and Infinite Distance
Objector raises three counterpoints: inseparable parts, infinite distance, and time analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second objection against the argument for divine oneness, including the objector's defense of composition with inseparable parts and his use of infinite distance and time analogy.

Core Idea
Composition is not impossible if parts are inseparable; infinite distance parallels infinite time.

In the previous lesson, the author argued that God cannot be composed of parts because composition entails dependence. Here, the objector responds by conceding that composition follows but denying its impossibility. He argues that parts might be necessarily inseparable, and then introduces a new dimension: the distance between God and the Throne. By distinguishing finite and infinite distances, and drawing an analogy with time, he attempts to show that the author's argument fails. This passage presents a sophisticated dialectical move that must be understood before proceeding to the author's reply.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage twice
First read for overall flow, noting the objector's concessions and assertions. Second reading: identify the three distinct moves he makes.
The objector starts by conceding composition but questions why that is impossible.
2
Identify the three parts
The objection has three parts: (1) composition with inseparable parts, (2) distinction between finite and infinite distance between God and the Throne, (3) response to the confinement objection using an analogy with time.
3
Analyze the time analogy
Consider how the objector parallels God's infinite past (pre-eternity) with infinite spatial distance. Ask: does this analogy hold? What does it reveal about the nature of divine existence?
The objector's key move is to argue that if infinite temporal separation is possible, why not infinite spatial separation?
4
Reflect on the overall strategy
Consider how the objector is using the author's own terms (infinite, finite, confinement) to construct a counter-argument. This shows the importance of precise definitions in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
سلمنا أنه يلزم منه كونه تعالى مركبا من الأجزاء والأبعاض . فلم قلتم : إن ذلك محال ؟ ولم لا يجوز أن يقال : إنه وإن كان مؤلفا من الأجزاء ، إلا أنها لا تقبل التفرق والتمزق بل هي واجبة البقاء على نسق واحد ؟ سلمنا أن ذلك باطل إلا أن هذا إنما يلزم ، إذا قلنا : إنه تعالى محاذ للعرش ببعد متناه أما إذا قلنا : إنه مباين للعرش ببعد غير متناه فهذا غير لازم . أما قوله ( لو كان كذلك ، لزم أن يكون ما لا نهاية له محصورابين حاصرين ، وذلك محال ، قلنا : أليس أن الله تعالى قديم أزلي ؟ فعلى هذا أنه لا أول لوجوده في جانب الأزل ، أما وجوده من الجانب الذي يلينا فإنه مقارن لوجود كل واحد من هذه الحوادث ، فإذا جاز في المدة والزمان أن تكون ذاته مباينة لهذه الحوادث، من جانب الأزل بينونة غير متناهية، مع أنه يكون مقارناً للعالم في جانب الأبد .
We concede that it follows from it that He is composed of parts and limbs. So why do you say that that is impossible? And why is it not permissible to say that He, although composed of parts, yet they do not accept separation and disintegration, but rather are necessarily remaining in one order? We concede that that is false, but this only follows if we say that He is opposite the Throne with a finite distance. But if we say that He is separate from the Throne with an infinite distance, then this does not follow. As for His saying: 'If it were so, then what has no end would be confined between two confiners, and that is impossible.' We say: 'Is not God eternal without beginning? Thus He has no beginning to His existence on the side of pre-eternity. As for His existence on the side that faces us, it is concurrent with the existence of each one of these events. So if it is permissible in duration and time that His essence be separate from these events from the side of pre-eternity with an infinite separation, while He is concurrent with the world on the side of eternity, then why is it not also permissible to say: His essence is separate from the Throne with an infinite distance?'
Source pages p. 52

Commentary
This passage is a second objection-and-answer. The objector concedes that the previous argument leads to God being composed of parts, but questions why composition is impossible. He suggests that even if composed, the parts might be inseparable. The author responds by first conceding that the composition argument only applies if God is opposite the Throne by finite distance; if infinite distance, it does not follow. The objector then raises a new point: if God is separate by infinite distance, then the distance would be infinite and confined between two limits, which is impossible. The author counters by drawing an analogy with time: God is separate from events in time by an infinite past (pre-eternity) while being concurrent with the world in the future. If that is possible in time, why not in distance? This shows the objector's argument is not necessarily valid.

Key Terms

أبعاض
بعد غير متناه
أزلي
أبد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Conceding composition means accepting that God has separable parts and is thus corporeal.
Correction: The objector explicitly states that the parts are inseparable (لا تقبل التفرق والتمزق) and remain in one order, so composition does not imply divisibility or corporeality in the usual sense.

Checkpoint
What analogy does the objector use to counter the claim that infinite separation would be confined between two limits?
A) God is like an indivisible atom.
B) God's existence in pre-eternity and eternity is analogous to infinite distance.
C) The Throne is also infinite.
D) The parts of God are inseparable.

Key Takeaway
The objector defends the possibility of divine composition by arguing that parts may be inseparable and by drawing an analogy between infinite spatial distance and infinite temporal distance.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The objector uses an analogy between infinite time and infinite space to defend his position. Have you ever used a clever analogy to justify a belief that deep down you knew might be flawed? What does this reveal about your own tendency to rationalize rather than submit to truth? How can you guard against your own reasoning becoming a barrier to accepting what is clear?
?
The objector concedes that his view might lead to composition but argues that parts could be inseparable. Is there an area of your faith where you hold a position simply because you cannot see the alternative, not because you have evidence? Are you willing to honestly examine whether you are clinging to an opinion out of intellectual pride rather than a genuine search for understanding? Commit to researching that issue with an open heart.
?
In this passage, the objector's argument depends on the possibility of infinite separation. When you think about God's transcendence, do you sometimes rely on abstract philosophical possibilities to avoid confronting the simplicity of His oneness? How can you move from intellectual defense of theological concepts to a deeper, humble awe of the Divine that silences such arguments?

8 min
The Objection of Infinite Separation and Direction
Initial objection and analysis of direction cases

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why infinite separation between God and the Throne cannot imply a specific direction without leading to absurdities.

Core Idea
Infinite distance does not escape direction; it still requires spatial location.

In this third objection, the author addresses a subtle argument: if God is infinitely distant from the Throne, does that put Him in a direction? The objection seems to avoid direction by using infinity, but the author shows it still implies a specific place. Let's follow his logical dismantling.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and initial answer
Read the Arabic passage from the start up to 'وحينئذ يلزم إبطال القول بإثبات الصانع وهو باطل'. Then read the corresponding translation. Notice the structure: an objection followed by a logical analysis.
The author immediately shifts to analyzing what it means to be 'specific to a place and direction'.
2
Identify the two cases for something in a direction
The author asks: does that thing exist in all six directions or not? These are the two possibilities. Note the reasoning for each.
This is a classic kalam method of dividing possibilities.
3
Understand the refutation of each case
If it does not exist in all directions, it becomes an indivisible atom (jawhar fard), impossible for God. If it does exist in all directions, then the same essence would be in multiple places at once, also impossible.
Both lead to contradictions, so the original objection fails.
4
Consider the reductio on the Throne
The author then applies the same reasoning to the Throne itself, showing that if the objector's logic were accepted, the Throne could be considered God—an absurdity.
This strengthens the refutation by showing the objection leads to further absurdities.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فلم لا يجوز أيضا أن يقال : إن ذاته مباينة للعرش ببعد غير متناه ، أنه يكون مختصا بالحيز والجهة ؟ والجواب : إنا إذا أشرنا إلى شيء مختص بحيز وجهة ، فقد حصل هناك شيء . وذلك الشيء هل له وجود في سائر الجوانب الستة أم لا ؟ أما الثاني فهو باطل . لأنه يلزم عليه أن يكون إله العالم جوهراً فرداً، وجزء لا يتجزأ؟ وذلك باطل بالاتفاق . يقي القسم الأول فنقول : الحاصل في كل واحد من الأحياز التي لا تنقسم . إما أن يكون عين الحاصل في الحيز الآخر أو غيره ؟ فإن كان الأول لزم أن يكون الذات الواحدة حاصلة في الأحياز الكثيرة دفعة واحدة ، وذلك معلوم الامتناع ببديهة العقل. وأيضاً : فلو عقل ما ذكرتم فلم لا يعقل أيضاً أن يقال: العرش جوهر فرد ، وجزء لا يتجزأ وهو بعينه حاصلاً في الأحياز الكثيرة ؟ وعلى هذا التقدير ، فلم يجوز أن يكون إله العالم هو نفس العرش ؟ وحينئذ يلزم إبطال القول بإثبات الصانع وهو باطل . قوله : « لم لا يجوز أن يكون مركبا من الأجزاء إلا أن تلك الأجزاء لا تقبل التفرق والتمزق ، ؟ قلنا : لأنا بينا أن الأجزاء الشاغلة للحيز ، يجب كونها متساوية في تمام الماهية ، وحينئذ يلزم جواز التفرق والتمزق ، ولأنه لو عقل ما ذكرتم ، فلم لا يجوز أن يقال : إله العالم هو العرش نفسه ؟ وهو وإن كان مركبا من الأجزاء والأبعاض إلا أن يقال : إله العالم هو العرش نفسه ؟ وهو وأن كان مركبا من الأجزاء والأبعاض إلا أنه لا يقبل التفرق والتمزق ، وحينئذ يلزم نفي الصانع وهو باطل . أما قوله « لم لا يجوز أن يكون الباري تعالى مباينا للعرش بينونة غير متناهية ) ؟ فنقول : هذا باطل . وذلك لأن ذات الباري تعالي ، لا بد وأن يكون متناهيا محدودا من جانب التحت وإلا لزم أن يكون العالم حاصلا في ذات الباري تعالى وهو محال بالاتفاق ، وإذا ثبت لذات الباري حد ونهاية من جانب التحت ، وثبت أيضا للعالم حد ونهاية ، فحينئذ يلزم أن يكون البعد بين الباري وبين العالم محدوداً بهذين الحدين ، وذلك [ لا ] يوجب كون ذلك البعد متناهيا ، فالقول بأنه غير متناه يوجب الجمع بين النقيضين وهو محال . وهذا بخلاف قولنا : إنه تعالى
Why is it not also permissible to say: His essence is separate from the Throne with an infinite distance, so that He becomes specific to a place and direction? The answer: If we point to something specific to a place and direction, then something has occurred there. Does that thing have existence in all six directions or not? The second (i.e., no) is false. Because it would entail that the God of the world is an indivisible atom and a part that cannot be divided, and that is false by consensus. Remains the first case, and we say: That which occurs in each one of the spaces that are indivisible – is it the same as that which occurs in the other space, or something else? If the first, then it would necessitate that the one essence occurs in many spaces at once, and that is known to be impossible by the necessity of the intellect. Also: if what you mentioned were intelligible, then why is it not also intelligible to say: the Throne is an indivisible atom and a part that cannot be divided, and it itself occurs in many spaces? On this assumption, why is it not permissible that the God of the world is the Throne itself? Then that would invalidate the statement affirming the Creator, and that is false. His saying: 'Why is it not permissible for Him to be composed of parts, but those parts do not accept separation and disintegration?' We say: Because we have shown that parts that occupy space must be equal in complete essence, and then separation and disintegration become possible. And because if what you mentioned were intelligible, then why is it not permissible to say: the God of the world is the Throne itself? And although it is composed of parts and limbs, it does not accept separation and disintegration. Then that would entail denying the Creator, which is false. As for His saying: 'Why is it not permissible for the Exalted Creator to be separate from the Throne with an infinite separation?' We say: This is false. That is because the essence of the Exalted Creator must be finite and limited from the underside, otherwise it would entail that the world occurs within the essence of the Exalted Creator, which is impossible by consensus. And if it is established that the essence of the Creator has a limit and end from the underside, and it is also established that the world has a limit and end, then the distance between the Creator and the world is necessarily limited by these two limits, and that [does not] necessitate that the distance be finite. So the statement that it is infinite necessitates combining two opposites, which is impossible. And this is contrary to our saying: He is Exalted.
Source pages p. 52

Commentary
This passage is a third objection-and-answer, actually containing multiple sub-objections. The objection: why can’t God be separate from the Throne by an infinite distance and thus be in a specific direction? The author answers by first analyzing the concept of being in a direction. If something is in a direction, it occupies a specific location. The author asks: does that something have existence in all six directions or not? If not, it becomes an indivisible atom (jawhar fard), which is impossible for God. If it does exist in all directions, then the same entity would be in multiple places at once, which is impossible by reason. Moreover, if we accept this, we could say the Throne itself is an atom present everywhere, making it God – absurd. Then the objector repeats the idea of composition without separability. The author responds by stating that parts occupying space must be essentially equal, so separability is possible; otherwise, if the Throne itself were composed of inseparable parts, it could be God – also absurd. Finally, the objection about infinite separation is refuted: God must have a limit from below (otherwise the world would be inside Him). Since both God (from below) and the world have limits, the distance between them is bounded by those limits, hence finite. Claiming it is infinite would be a contradiction. The author ends by distinguishing this from the earlier analogy in time, which did not involve boundaries.

Key Terms
حيز
جهة
جوهر فرد
جزء لا يتجزأ

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Infinite distance avoids assigning a direction to God
Correction: The passage shows that infinite distance still implies being in a specific direction and leads to spatial contradictions.

Checkpoint
What does the author first consider when analyzing something 'specific to a place and direction'?
A) Whether it is composite or simple.
B) Whether it exists in all six directions or not.
C) Whether it is infinite or finite.
D) Whether it is the Throne or not.

Key Takeaway
Even infinite separation cannot remove God from the category of spatial objects; it still leads to contradictions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you imagine Allah’s closeness or distance, do you ever subconsciously picture a physical space? What specific thought will you replace that with after this lesson?
?
If you truly believe Allah is not confined to any direction, does your dua reflect that? Are there times you face a specific direction out of habit rather than sincere orientation?
?
The passage brutally dismantles any attempt to assign spatial attributes to Allah. Is there a comfortable image of Allah you need to let go of? What will you do today to purify your tawheed?

Refuting Composition and Infinite Distance
Refutation of composition without separability and of infinite distance

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why God cannot be composed of inseparable parts and why the distance between God and the world must be finite.

Core Idea
Parts that occupy space must be separable; infinite distance requires finite limits.

The objector continues with two more attempts: first, that God could be composed of parts that don't separate, and second, that infinite separation avoids direction. The author refutes both: parts occupying space necessarily allow separation, and infinite distance is impossible because both God and the world have limits that bound any distance.

Guided Path
1
Read the composition objection and response
Read the section starting from 'قوله : « لم لا يجوز أن يكون مركبا من الأجزاء' up to the end of that response. Notice how the author argues that parts occupying space must be equal in essence and thus separable.
The author uses a reductio: if inseparable parts were possible, then the Throne itself could be God.
2
Read the infinite distance objection and final refutation
Read the section starting from 'أما قوله « لم لا يجوز أن يكون الباري تعالى مباينا للعرش بينونة غير متناهية ) ؟' to the end. The author argues that God must have a limit from below, and the world has a limit, so the distance is bounded.
The final sentence is incomplete but the argument is clear.
3
Understand the concept of limits
The author points out that if God had no limit from below, the world would be inside God, which is impossible. Therefore, God has a limit, and the world has a limit, so the distance between them is finite.
This is a key point in kalam about God's transcendence and spatial reasoning.
4
Connect the refutation to the earlier time analogy
The author ends by contrasting this spatial argument with the earlier one about time, which did not involve boundaries.
This shows the consistency of the overall argument.

Why does the author argue that the distance between God and the world must be finite?
A) Because the world is infinite.
B) Because both God’s essence (from below) and the world have limits that bound the distance.
C) Because the Throne is the limit of the universe.
D) Because God is infinite in all directions.

Key Takeaway
Any spatial distance between God and creation must be finite, because both have limits that define the boundaries.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In this lesson, you learned that God cannot be infinitely distant from creation. When you feel like God is far away from you, what do you really mean? Is it a physical distance or something else? Will you now stop using spatial metaphors to excuse spiritual neglect?
?
The argument shows that if God had no limit from below, the world would be inside Him. How does this truth about God's transcendence and immanence challenge the way you think about His nearness to you? What will you do today to actually feel His closeness without imagining it physically?
?
You accepted that parts occupying space must be separable, or else the Throne could be God. Have you ever held an idea about God that, upon honest examination, reduces Him to something physical or limited? What step will you take to purify your belief?

The Eleventh Proof: Absurdity of Divine Location
Rāzī presents the eleventh proof against God's location using a reductio ad absurdum.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why attributing a spatial location to God leads to absurd consequences, and how this proof refutes the view that God occupies a place or direction.

Core Idea
If God were located, He would be either divisible or solid—both absurd.

In this proof, Fakhr al-Dīn al-Rāzī presents a powerful reductio ad absurdum argument against the idea that God has a spatial location. By considering the implications of God being in a specific place—either penetrable or impenetrable—he shows that such a view leads to conclusions incompatible with divinity. This passage is a clear example of rational theological reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof's structure
Start by reading the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the conditional reasoning: 'if... then either... or...'
2
Identify the two branches
The argument splits into two possibilities: penetration possible or not. Identify what each implies about God's nature.
3
Grasp the conclusion
See how the argument reduces the position of those who affirm location to absurdity—God becomes either a thin divisible substance or a solid rock.
4
Reflect on the implication
Understand why this proof is considered effective in Islamic theology—it shows that location is incompatible with God's transcendence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الحادية عشر : إنه تعالى لو كان حاصلا في حيز معين لكنا إذا فرضنا وصول إنسان إلى طرف ذلك الشيء ، فإما أن يمكنه الدخول فيه ، والنفوذ فيه وإما أن لا يمكن، فإن كان الأول كان كالهواء اللطيف ، والماء اللطيف ، فحينئذ يكون قابلا للتفرق والتمزق ، وإن كان الثاني فحينئذ يكون صلبا كالحجر الصلد الذي يمتنع النفوذ فيه ، فيرجع حاصل هذا القول : إلى أن إله العالم عند القائلين بالمكان والجهة : شيء رقيق كالماء والهواء [ أو شيء صلب كالحجر (۱) ] فيكون [ هذا القابل أما الهواء اللطيف وأما الحجر الصلب (۲) ] فيكون حاصل قوله : إنه حصل فوق العرش حجر صلب : جاري مجرى جبل عظيم ، وهو بعينه (۳) ذلك الجبل . ومعلوم أن هذا القول بعيد عن العقل جدا .
The eleventh proof: If the Exalted were contained in a specific place, then if we suppose a person reaching the boundary of that thing, either it is possible for him to enter it and penetrate it, or it is not possible. If the first, then it would be like fine air or fine water, and then it would be susceptible to division and tearing. If the second, then it would be solid like a hard stone which cannot be penetrated. So the outcome of this statement is that the god of the world according to those who affirm location and direction is either a thin thing like water and air, or a solid thing like stone. So the conclusion of his statement is that above the Throne there is a solid stone, similar to a great mountain, and it is exactly that mountain. And it is known that this statement is far from reason.
Source pages p. 54

Commentary
This passage is a proof (argument) against the attribution of spatial location to God. The author (Fakhr al-Dīn al-Rāzī) presents a reductio ad absurdum. He argues: if God were in a specific place, then we could consider a person reaching the boundary of that place. Either that person can penetrate into it or not. If yes, then God would be like air or water, subject to division and tearing. If no, then God would be solid like a rock, impenetrable. The conclusion is that those who affirm location for God end up either with a thin, divisible deity or a hard, rock-like deity, which is obviously absurd. The lesson contains the complete argument and its conclusion.

Key Terms
حيز
جهة
تفرق
تمزق
صلب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God is located in heaven or above the throne.
Correction: This proof shows that location implies material properties, which are impossible for God. God is beyond space and direction.

Checkpoint
According to the eleventh proof, what are the two possible consequences if God were in a specific place?
A) He would be either divisible (like air) or solid (like stone).
B) He would be either visible or invisible.
C) He would be either finite or infinite.
D) He would be either eternal or temporal.

Key Takeaway
The eleventh proof demonstrates that attributing location to God inevitably leads to describing Him in physical terms, which is absurd and contradicts His transcendence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you picture Allah in your mind during worship, do you inadvertently imagine Him as being 'up' or in a specific direction? How does this proof challenge that mental image, and what concrete step will you take to purify your conception of Allah?
?
Have you ever felt spiritually distant because you thought Allah was 'far away' in a spatial sense? How can the logic of this proof help you replace that feeling with the awareness that His transcendence means He is beyond all physical distance, yet closer than your jugular vein?
?
Is there a place or time where you feel Allah's presence more than others (e.g., mosque, night prayer)? While these moments are blessings, do they make you unconsciously limit Him? What is one change you will make to ensure you worship Allah as the Transcendent, not as confined to any space?

The Negative Correlation Between Corporeality and Power
Rāzī inductively observes a negative correlation between corporeality and power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Rāzī's inductive observation that greater corporeality correlates with weaker effective power, as illustrated by the elements and celestial spheres.

Core Idea
The denser the body, the weaker its power to affect others.

Rāzī begins his twelfth proof by presenting an inductive pattern derived from observing natural bodies. He argues that as corporeality decreases, effective power increases. This lesson walks through the examples from earth to the celestial spheres that establish this negative correlation.

Guided Path
1
Read the inductive principle
Start by reading the opening statement that sets up the correlation: the stronger the corporeality, the weaker the power.
This is the key claim of the proof.
2
Examine the examples
Go through each example: earth, water, air, fire, and celestial spheres. Notice how each is less dense than the previous and more powerful.
Pay attention to the order: from most corporeal to least.
3
Reflect on the pattern
Ask yourself: What pattern emerges from these observations? The proof relies on this pattern as a general rule.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثانية عشر : وهي حجة استقرائية اعتبارية - فنقول : إنا رأينا أن الشيء كلما كان حصول معنى الجسمية فيه أقوى وأكمل ، كانت القوة الفاعلة المؤثرة فيه أضعف وأنقص . وكلما كان حصول معنى الجسمية فيه أنقص وأضعف ، كان حصول القوة الفاعلة فيه أقوى وأكمل ، وتقريره : إنا وجدنا الأرض أكثف الأجسام وأقواها حجمية ، فلا جرم لم يحصل فيها إلا قبول الأثر فقط ، فأما أن يكون للأرض الخالية تأثير في غيره ، فقليل جداً . وأما الماء فهو أقل كثافة وحجمية من الأرض ، فلا جرم حصلت فيه قوة مؤثرة ، فإن الماء جاري بطبعه ، فإذا اختلط بالأرض أثر فيه أنواعاً من التأثيرات وأما الهواء فإنـه أقل حجمية وكثافة من الماء ، فلا جرم كان أقوى في التأثير من الماء . ولذلك قال بعضهم : : إن الحياة لا تكمل إلا بالنفس ، وزعموا : أنه لا معنى للروح إلا الهواء المستنشق ، وأما النار فإنها الطف من الهواء ، فلا جرم كانت أقوى الأجسام العنصرية في التأثير، فإن بقوة الحرارة يحصل الطبخ والنضج، وتكون المواليد الثلاثة - أعني : المعادن والحيوان والنبات - وأما الأفلاك فإنها لما كانت الطف من الأجرام العنصرية ، لا جرم كانت هي المستولية على هذه العناصر بالتمزيج والتركيب ، وهذا على قول أكثر الفلاسفة المتقدمين الذين يقولون : أجرام الأفلاك ليست صلبية ، بل هي جارية مجرى الأنوار اللطيفة . وإذا ثبت هذا . فنقول : هذا الاستقراء يدل على أن الشيء كلما كان أكثر جرمية وحجماً كان أقل قوة وتأثيراً ، وكلما كان أقوى قوة وتأثيرا ، كان أقل حجمية وجرمية ، وإذا كان كذلك فهذا الاستقراء يفيد ظنا قويا أنه حيث حصل كمال القوة والقدرة على الإحداث والإيجاد، وجب أن لا يحصل هناك معنى الحجمية والجرمية ، والاختصاص بالحيز والجهة ، وهذا وإن كان بحثا استقرائيا ، إلا أنه عند الاعتبار العام ، يفيد كونه سبحانه منزها عن الجسمية ، والاختصاص بالحيز ، والجهة . [ والله أعلم (۱) ]
The twelfth proof: It is an inductive and reflective proof. We say: We observe that whenever the meaning of corporeality in a thing is stronger and more complete, the effective power in it is weaker and deficient. And whenever the meaning of corporeality in it is weaker and deficient, the possession of effective power in it is stronger and more complete. Its exposition: We find earth is the densest of bodies and strongest in size, so it only receives effect, and its influence on others is very little. As for water, it is less dense and of lesser size than earth, so it possesses effective power—water flows naturally, and when it mixes with earth it affects it with various influences. As for air, it is even lesser in size and density than water, so it is stronger in influence than water. That is why some said that life is only completed by the breath, and they claimed that spirit is nothing but inhaled air. As for fire, it is subtler than air, so it is the strongest of elemental bodies in influence, for by the power of heat cooking and ripening occur, and the three kinds of generated things—minerals, animals, and plants—are produced. As for the celestial spheres, since they are subtler than elemental bodies, they dominate these elements by mixing and composition, according to the view of most earlier philosophers who say that the celestial bodies are not solid, but are like fine lights. And once this is established, we say: This induction indicates that the more corporeal and voluminous a thing is, the less power and influence it has, and the stronger in power and influence it is, the less size and corporeality it has. And if so, this induction yields a strong presumption that where there is perfect power and ability to originate and create, there must be no meaning of size and corporeality or being located in a place and direction. And although this is an inductive inquiry, nevertheless in general consideration it benefits the conclusion that He is exalted above corporeality and being located in a place and direction. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 55

Commentary
This passage is an inductive argument (استقراء) linking corporeality with weakness. The author observes a negative correlation: the more corporeal and dense an object is, the less power it has to affect others. He provides examples: earth (most corporeal, least effective), water (less dense, more effective), air (even less dense, more effective), fire (subtler, very effective), and celestial spheres (subtlest, dominate the elements). From this induction, he concludes that the being with absolute power and ability to create must be completely free from corporeality and spatial location. Although the argument is probabilistic, it yields strong conviction. The lesson contains the full induction and its conclusion.

Key Terms
استقراء
جسمية
قوة فاعلة
عناصر
أفلاك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that dense bodies like earth have great power because they are solid.
Correction: Rāzī shows that density correlates with passivity, not power. The most corporeal bodies have the least influence.

Checkpoint
According to the inductive examples, which of the following has the most effective power?
A) Earth
B) Water
C) Fire
D) Air

Key Takeaway
Rāzī uses a graded series of natural bodies to establish that the less corporeal a thing is, the more power it possesses.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have a physical body. Where in your life do you rely most on bodily strength, status, or material security rather than spiritual power? What would it take to shift your reliance toward the non-corporeal, even if it feels weaker?
?
When you see the earth, you think of it as solid and immovable. But Rāzī says it is weakest in power. How does that challenge your own assumptions about what 'strength' really means? Are you building your life on something as fragile as earth?
?
If the most subtle bodies have the most power, how does that reshape your understanding of your own soul? Are you feeding its subtlety through knowledge and worship, or are you making it dense with worldly attachments? What one concrete change can you make today to lighten your soul?

From Induction to Divine Transcendence
Rāzī concludes the inductive proof by denying God's corporeality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Rāzī's inductive observation leads to the conclusion that the absolutely powerful Creator must be free from corporeality and spatial location.

Core Idea
Perfect power requires absolute non-corporeality.

Having established the inverse correlation between corporeality and power, Rāzī now draws the conclusion. He argues that since God possesses perfect power to originate and create, He must be completely free from volume, corporeality, and being confined to a place. This lesson explains the final step of the proof.

Guided Path
1
Review the inductive generalization
Recall from the previous passage the pattern: more corporeality means less power. Now, see how Rāzī generalizes this into a universal principle.
The induction is probabilistic but strong.
2
Apply the principle to God
God has perfect power to create. According to the pattern, such power requires minimal corporeality. In fact, it requires none.
This is an argument by extrapolation.
3
Embrace the conclusion
The conclusion is that God is transcendent above corporeality, place, and direction. Acknowledge the probabilistic nature but note its strength.

Source Text

Are you willing to base your life on strong presumption where revelation is clear, or do you secretly demand absolute proof before committing? ?

Divine Transcendence
intermediate

The Inductive Argument for God's Transcendence of Corporeality
Inductive argument from elements to God's transcendence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how an inductive study of the physical elements leads to the conclusion that God is free from body, space, and direction.

Core Idea
The more power a thing has, the less corporeal it is; God's absolute power means He is beyond matter.

This lesson presents a fascinating inductive argument (istiqra') from the natural world. By examining the relationship between density and effectiveness in the physical elements, the author derives a pattern that points to the transcendence of the Creator. The passage uses empirical observation to support a core theological doctrine: that God is free from corporeality, location, and direction. Let's follow the reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Follow the inductive survey of elements
The passage examines earth, water, air, fire, and celestial spheres, noting a pattern: as bulk decreases, influence increases.
This is based on the Aristotelian-Ptolemaic cosmos familiar to classical scholars.
2
Note the key principle derived
Power and corporeality are inversely related. The more power, the less volume.
3
Apply the principle to the case of ultimate power
Since God has perfect power to create, He must be free from all bulk and spatial confinement.
The author calls this a 'strong presumption' (zann qawiyy), not certainty.
4
Recognize the nature of the argument
This is an inductive inquiry (bahth istiqra'i), not a demonstrative proof, but it strongly supports transcendence.
The argument does not claim absolute certainty, but it is persuasive on general consideration.

فقط ، فأما أن يكون للأرض الخالية تأثير في غيره ، فقليل جداً . وأما الماء فهو أقل كثافة وحجمية من الأرض ، فلا جرم حصلت فيه قوة مؤثرة ، فإن الماء جاري بطبعه ، فإذا اختلط بالأرض أثر فيه أنواعاً من التأثيرات وأما الهواء فإنـه أقل حجمية وكثافة من الماء ، فلا جرم كان أقوى في التأثير من الماء . ولذلك قال بعضهم : : إن الحياة لا تكمل إلا بالنفس ، وزعموا : أنه لا معنى للروح إلا الهواء المستنشق ، وأما النار فإنها الطف من الهواء ، فلا جرم كانت أقوى الأجسام العنصرية في التأثير، فإن بقوة الحرارة يحصل الطبخ والنضج، وتكون المواليد الثلاثة - أعني : المعادن والحيوان والنبات - وأما الأفلاك فإنها لما كانت الطف من الأجرام العنصرية ، لا جرم كانت هي المستولية على هذه العناصر بالتمزيج والتركيب ، وهذا على قول أكثر الفلاسفة المتقدمين الذين يقولون : أجرام الأفلاك ليست صلبية ، بل هي جارية مجرى الأنوار اللطيفة . وإذا ثبت هذا . فنقول : هذا الاستقراء يدل على أن الشيء كلما كان أكثر جرمية وحجماً كان أقل قوة وتأثيراً ، وكلما كان أقوى قوة وتأثيرا ، كان أقل حجمية وجرمية ، وإذا كان كذلك فهذا الاستقراء يفيد ظنا قويا أنه حيث حصل کمال القوة والقدرة على الإحداث والإيجاد، وجب أن لا يحصل هناك معنى الحجمية والجرمية ، والاختصاص بالحيز والجهة ، وهذا وإن كان بحثا استقرائيا ، إلا أنه عند الاعتبار العام ، يفيد كونه سبحانه منزها عن الجسمية ، والاختصاص بالحيز ، والجهة . والله أعلم
Only, as for bare earth having influence on something else, it is very little. As for water, it is less dense and voluminous than earth, so inevitably it acquired effective power, for water flows by its nature; when it mixes with earth it imparts various influences on it. As for air, it is less voluminous and dense than water, so inevitably it was stronger in influence than water. For that reason some said: life is not complete except with breath (nafs), and they claimed: there is no meaning of spirit (ruh) except inhaled air. As for fire, it is subtler than air, so inevitably it was the strongest of the elementary bodies in influence, for by the power of heat cooking and ripening occur, and the three productions – namely minerals, animals, and plants – come to be. As for the celestial spheres, since they were subtler than the elementary bodies, inevitably they were the ones dominating these elements through mixing and composition; and this is according to the opinion of most of the earlier philosophers who say: the bodies of the spheres are not solid, but rather run on the pattern of subtle lights. And when this is established, we say: this induction indicates that the more a thing has bulk and volume, the less power and influence it has; and the more powerful and influential it is, the less volume and bulk it has. And if it is so, then this induction yields a strong presumption that where perfection of power and ability to create and originate is attained, it is necessary that there not occur the meaning of bulk and volume, and being confined to space and direction. And although this is an inductive inquiry, yet upon general consideration it yields that He, exalted, is transcendent of corporeality, and of being confined to space and direction. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 55

Commentary
This passage presents an inductive argument (istiqra') for the transcendence of God (tanzih) above corporeality, location, and direction. The author begins by examining the physical elements – earth, water, air, fire, and celestial spheres – noting an inverse relationship between corporeality (jirmiyya/hajmiyya) and effective power (quwwa ta'thir). Earth, being most dense and voluminous, has almost no influence on others. Water is less dense and more influential; air even more so; fire still stronger; and the celestial spheres, being subtlest, dominate the elements. This empirical pattern (istiqra') suggests that as a thing's power and ability to create (al-qudra 'ala al-ihdath wa al-ijad) reaches perfection, it must be free from volume, bulk, spatial location (hayyiz), and direction (jiha). The conclusion is that this reasoning, though inductive, supports the doctrine that God is free (munazzah) from all corporeality. The author ends with 'wa Allah a'lam', acknowledging that ultimate knowledge belongs to God.

Key Terms
استقراء (istiqra')
جرمية (jirmiyya)
قوة تأثير (quwwa ta'thir)
حيز (hayyiz)
جهة (jiha)
تنزيه (tanzih)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This is a definitive rational proof (burhan qat'i) for God's incorporeality.
Correction: The author explicitly calls it an inductive inquiry (bahth istiqra'i) yielding only a strong presumption (zann qawiyy), not logical certainty.

Checkpoint
What does the author conclude from the inductive examination of the elements?
A) The celestial spheres are made of solid matter.
B) God's perfection of power implies that He has no body, space, or direction.
C) Fire is the strongest element and therefore God is like fire.
D) The elements prove that God is corporeal.

Key Takeaway
Through an inductive study of the physical elements, we see that greater power corresponds to less corporeality, and this points to the transcendence of God from body, space, and direction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You are made of these physical elements and limited by them. How does knowing that the One you worship is completely free from all bulk and space change the way you direct your prayers? Do you ever subconsciously imagine Allah in a place or direction? What can you do today to purify your conception of Him?
?
The inductive principle taught that influence increases as physical density decreases. In your own life, what material attachments or physical habits are making you spiritually 'dense' and less effective in worship? Name one attachment you will try to reduce this week to increase your spiritual influence.
?
This argument shows that true power is inversely related to physical bulk. How does that contrast with the way the world measures strength? Are you more impressed by worldly might or by spiritual power? What steps can you take to realign your values with this truth?
